</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Website</th><td class="infobox-data"><span class="url"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://aws.amazon.com/">aws<wbr />.amazon<wbr />.com</a></span></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Amazon Web Services, Inc.</b> (<b>AWS</b>) is a subsidiary of <a href="/wiki/Amazon_(company)" title="Amazon (company)">Amazon</a> that provides <a href="/wiki/Software_as_a_service" title="Software as a service">on-demand</a> <a href="/wiki/Cloud_computing" title="Cloud computing">cloud computing</a> <a href="/wiki/Computing_platform" title="Computing platform">platforms</a> and <a href="/wiki/API" title="API">APIs</a> to individuals, companies, and governments, on a metered, pay-as-you-go basis.
</p><p>Clients often use this in combination with <a href="/wiki/Elasticity_(system_resource)" class="mw-redirect" title="Elasticity (system resource)">autoscaling</a> (a process that allows a client to use more computing in times of high application usage, and then scale down to reduce costs when there is less traffic). These cloud computing <a href="/wiki/Web_services" class="mw-redirect" title="Web services">web services</a> provide various services related to networking, compute, storage, <a href="/wiki/Middleware" title="Middleware">middleware</a>, <a href="/wiki/Internet_of_things" title="Internet of things">IoT</a> and other processing capacity, as well as <a href="/wiki/Software_tools" class="mw-redirect" title="Software tools">software tools</a> via AWS <a href="/wiki/Server_farms" class="mw-redirect" title="Server farms">server farms</a>.  This frees clients from managing, scaling, and patching hardware and operating systems.
</p><p>One of the foundational services is <a href="/wiki/Amazon_Elastic_Compute_Cloud" title="Amazon Elastic Compute Cloud">Amazon Elastic Compute Cloud</a> (EC2), which allows users to have at their disposal a <a href="/wiki/Virtualization" title="Virtualization">virtual</a> <a href="/wiki/Computer_cluster" title="Computer cluster">cluster of computers</a>, with extremely high availability, which can be interacted with over the internet via <a href="/wiki/REST" title="REST">REST</a> APIs, a <a href="/wiki/Command-line_interface" title="Command-line interface">CLI</a> or the AWS console.  AWS's virtual computers emulate most of the attributes of a real computer, including hardware <a href="/wiki/Central_processing_unit" title="Central processing unit">central processing units</a> (CPUs) and <a href="/wiki/Graphics_processing_unit" title="Graphics processing unit">graphics processing units</a> (GPUs) for processing; local/<a href="/wiki/Random-access_memory" title="Random-access memory">RAM</a> memory; <a href="/wiki/Hard_disk_drive" title="Hard disk drive">hard-disk</a> (HDD)/<a href="/wiki/Solid-state_drive" title="Solid-state drive">SSD storage</a>; a choice of <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating systems</a>; networking; and pre-loaded application software such as <a href="/wiki/Web_server" title="Web server">web servers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Database" title="Database">databases</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Customer_relationship_management" title="Customer relationship management">customer relationship management</a> (CRM).
</p><p>AWS services are delivered to customers via a network of AWS server farms located throughout the world. Fees are based on a combination of usage (known as a "Pay-as-you-go" model), hardware, operating system, software, and networking features chosen by the subscriber requiring various degrees of <a href="/wiki/Availability_(system)" title="Availability (system)">availability</a>, <a href="/wiki/Redundancy_(engineering)" title="Redundancy (engineering)">redundancy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Computer_security" title="Computer security">security</a>, and service options. Subscribers can pay for a single virtual AWS computer, a dedicated physical computer, or clusters of either.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-AWSagreement&#95;9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AWSagreement-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Amazon provides select portions of security for subscribers (e.g. physical security of the data centers) while other aspects of security are the responsibility of the subscriber (e.g. account management, vulnerability scanning, patching). AWS operates from many global geographical regions, including nine in North America.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-GlobalInfrastructure&#95;10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GlobalInfrastructure-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Amazon markets AWS to subscribers as a way of obtaining large-scale computing capacity more quickly and cheaply than building an actual physical server farm.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> All services are billed based on usage, but each service measures usage in varying ways. As of 2023 Q1, AWS has 31% market share for cloud infrastructure while the next two competitors <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Azure" title="Microsoft Azure">Microsoft Azure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Google_Cloud_Platform" title="Google Cloud Platform">Google Cloud</a> have 25%, and 11% respectively, according to Synergy Research Group.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Services">Services</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amazon_Web_Services&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Services"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Amazon_Elastic_Compute_Cloud" title="Amazon Elastic Compute Cloud">Amazon Elastic Compute Cloud</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amazon_S3" title="Amazon S3">Amazon S3</a></div>
<p>As of 2025<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Amazon_Web_Services&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> AWS comprises over 200<sup id="cite&#95;ref-techradar&#95;14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-techradar-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> products and services including <a href="/wiki/Computation" title="Computation">computing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amazon_Elastic_File_System" title="Amazon Elastic File System">storage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Computer_network" title="Computer network">networking</a>, <a href="/wiki/Database" title="Database">database</a>, <a href="/wiki/Analytics" title="Analytics">analytics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Application_service_provider" title="Application service provider">application services</a>, <a href="/wiki/Software_deployment" title="Software deployment">deployment</a>, <a href="/wiki/Systems_management" title="Systems management">management</a>, <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">machine learning</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mobile_application_development" class="mw-redirect" title="Mobile application development">mobile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Programming_tool" title="Programming tool">developer tools</a>, RobOps and tools for the <a href="/wiki/Internet_of_Things" class="mw-redirect" title="Internet of Things">Internet of Things</a>. The most popular include <a href="/wiki/Amazon_Elastic_Compute_Cloud" title="Amazon Elastic Compute Cloud">Amazon Elastic Compute Cloud</a> (EC2), <a href="/wiki/Amazon_Simple_Storage_Service" class="mw-redirect" title="Amazon Simple Storage Service">Amazon Simple Storage Service</a> (Amazon S3), Amazon Connect, and <a href="/wiki/AWS_Lambda" title="AWS Lambda">AWS Lambda</a> (a <a href="/wiki/Serverless_computing" title="Serverless computing">serverless function</a> that can perform arbitrary code written in any <a href="/wiki/Programming_language" title="Programming language">language</a> that can be configured to be triggered by hundreds of <a href="/wiki/Event_(computing)" title="Event (computing)">events</a>, including <a href="/wiki/HTTP" title="HTTP">HTTP</a> calls).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-mostpopularservices&#95;16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mostpopularservices-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Services expose functionality through APIs for clients to use in their applications.  These APIs are accessed over HTTP, using the <a href="/wiki/REST" title="REST">REST</a> architectural style and <a href="/wiki/SOAP" title="SOAP">SOAP</a> protocol for older APIs and exclusively <a href="/wiki/JSON" title="JSON">JSON</a> for newer ones. Clients can interact with these APIs in various ways, including from the AWS console (a website), by using <a href="/wiki/Software_development_kit" title="Software development kit">SDKs</a> written in various languages (such as <a href="/wiki/Python_(programming_language)" title="Python (programming language)">Python</a>, <a href="/wiki/Java_(programming_language)" title="Java (programming language)">Java</a>, and <a href="/wiki/JavaScript" title="JavaScript">JavaScript</a>), or by making direct REST calls.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amazon_Web_Services&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Amazon_Web_Services" title="Timeline of Amazon Web Services">Timeline of Amazon Web Services</a></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Founding_(2000–2005)"><span id="Founding_.282000.E2.80.932005.29"></span>Founding (2000–2005)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amazon_Web_Services&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Founding (2000–2005)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Amazon.com_web_services_2002.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5d/Amazon.com_web_services_2002.jpg/250px-Amazon.com_web_services_2002.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="180" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/5/5d/Amazon.com_web_services_2002.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="316" data-file-height="316" /></a><figcaption>Early AWS "building blocks" logo along a <a href="/wiki/Sigmoid_curve" class="mw-redirect" title="Sigmoid curve">sigmoid curve</a> depicting <a href="/wiki/Recession_shapes#V-shaped" title="Recession shapes">recession</a> followed by growth<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>The genesis of AWS came in the early 2000s. After building <i>Merchant.com</i>, Amazon's e-commerce-as-a-service platform that offers third-party retailers a way to build their own web-stores, Amazon pursued <a href="/wiki/Service-oriented_architecture" title="Service-oriented architecture">service-oriented architecture</a> as a means to scale its engineering operations,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-horsemouth&#95;17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-horsemouth-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-foraws&#95;18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-foraws-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-tcaws&#95;19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tcaws-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-crnaws&#95;20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crnaws-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-hilabs&#95;21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hilabs-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-vdynamo&#95;22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vdynamo-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-vogels&#95;23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vogels-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> led by then <a href="/wiki/Chief_technology_officer" title="Chief technology officer">CTO</a> Allan Vermeulen.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-brad&#95;24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brad-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Around the same time frame, Amazon was frustrated with the speed of its software engineering, and sought to implement various recommendations put forth by Matt Round, an engineering leader at the time, including maximization of autonomy for engineering teams, adoption of <a href="/wiki/Representational_state_transfer" class="mw-redirect" title="Representational state transfer">REST</a>, standardization of infrastructure, removal of gate-keeping decision-makers (bureaucracy), and <a href="/wiki/Continuous_deployment" title="Continuous deployment">continuous deployment</a>. He also called for increasing the percentage of the time engineers spent building the software rather than doing other tasks.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Amazon created "a shared <a href="/wiki/Information_Technology" class="mw-redirect" title="Information Technology">IT</a> platform" so its engineering organizations, which were spending 70% of their time on "undifferentiated heavy-lifting" such as IT and infrastructure problems, could focus on customer-facing innovation instead.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-aselip&#95;27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aselip-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Besides, in dealing with unusual peak traffic patterns, especially during <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_Christmas" title="Economics of Christmas">the holiday season</a>, by migrating services to commodity Linux hardware and relying on <a href="/wiki/Open_source_software" class="mw-redirect" title="Open source software">open source software</a>, Amazon's Infrastructure team, led by Tom Killalea,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-tompeak&#95;28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tompeak-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Amazon's first <a href="/wiki/CISO" class="mw-redirect" title="CISO">CISO</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> had already run its data centers and associated services in a "fast, reliable, cheap" way.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-tompeak&#95;28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tompeak-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In July 2002 Amazon.com Web Services, managed by Colin Bryar,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> launched its first <a href="/wiki/Web_service" title="Web service">web services</a>, opening up the Amazon.com platform to all developers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Over one hundred applications were built on top of it by 2004.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-tomlinux&#95;32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tomlinux-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  This unexpected developer interest took Amazon by surprise and convinced them that developers were "hungry for more".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-aselip&#95;27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aselip-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>By the summer of 2003, <a href="/wiki/Andy_Jassy" title="Andy Jassy">Andy Jassy</a> had taken over Bryar's portfolio<sup id="cite&#95;ref-working&#95;33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-working-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> at <a href="/wiki/Rick_Dalzell" title="Rick Dalzell">Rick Dalzell</a>'s behest, after Vermeulen, who was Bezos' first pick, declined the offer.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-brad&#95;24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brad-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Jassy subsequently mapped out the vision for an "Internet <a href="/wiki/Operating_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Operating System">OS</a>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-horsemouth&#95;17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-horsemouth-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-tcaws&#95;19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tcaws-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-hilabs&#95;21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hilabs-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-telaws&#95;34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-telaws-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> made up of foundational infrastructure primitives that alleviated key impediments to shipping software applications faster.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-horsemouth&#95;17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-horsemouth-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-foraws&#95;18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-foraws-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-tcaws&#95;19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tcaws-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-hilabs&#95;21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hilabs-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-vogels&#95;23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vogels-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By fall 2003,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-horsemouth&#95;17-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-horsemouth-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-tcaws&#95;19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tcaws-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Databases" class="mw-redirect" title="Databases">databases</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cloud_storage" title="Cloud storage">storage</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Compute" class="mw-redirect" title="Compute">compute</a> were identified as the first set of infrastructure pieces that Amazon should launch.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-horsemouth&#95;17-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-horsemouth-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-tcaws&#95;19-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tcaws-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-aselip&#95;27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aselip-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Jeff Barr, an early AWS employee, credits himself, Vermeulen, Jassy, Bezos, and a few others for coming up with the idea that would evolve into <a href="/wiki/Amazon_EC2" class="mw-redirect" title="Amazon EC2">EC2</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amazon_S3" title="Amazon S3">S3</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Amazon_RDS" class="mw-redirect" title="Amazon RDS">RDS</a>;<sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Jassy recalls the idea was the result of brainstorming for about a week with "ten of the best <a href="/wiki/Software_engineering" title="Software engineering">technology</a> minds and ten of the best <a href="/wiki/Product_management" title="Product management">product management</a> minds" on about ten different internet applications and the most primitive building blocks required to build them.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-hilabs&#95;21-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hilabs-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Werner_Vogels" title="Werner Vogels">Werner Vogels</a> cites Amazon's desire to make the process of "invent, launch, reinvent, relaunch, start over, rinse, repeat" as fast as it could was leading them to break down <a href="/wiki/Big_company" class="mw-redirect" title="Big company">organizational structures</a> with "two-pizza teams"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>c<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Software_architecture" title="Software architecture">application structures</a> with <a href="/wiki/Distributed_computing" title="Distributed computing">distributed systems</a>;<sup id="cite&#95;ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>d<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and that these changes ultimately paved way for the formation of AWS<sup id="cite&#95;ref-vogels&#95;23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vogels-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and its mission "to expose all of the atomic-level pieces of the Amazon.com platform".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Brewster_Kahle" title="Brewster Kahle">Brewster Kahle</a>, co-founder of <a href="/wiki/Alexa_Internet" title="Alexa Internet">Alexa Internet</a>, which was acquired by Amazon in 1999, his start-up's compute infrastructure helped Amazon solve its <a href="/wiki/Big_data" title="Big data">big data</a> problems and later informed the innovations that underpinned AWS.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Jassy assembled a founding team of 57 employees from a mix of engineering and business backgrounds to kick-start these initiatives,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-hilabs&#95;21-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hilabs-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-crnaws&#95;20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crnaws-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> with a majority of the hires coming from outside the company.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-hilabs&#95;21-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hilabs-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They included Jeff Lawson, the <a href="/wiki/Twilio" title="Twilio">Twilio</a> CEO;<sup id="cite&#95;ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Adam Selipsky, the <a href="/wiki/Tableau_Inc" class="mw-redirect" title="Tableau Inc">Tableau</a> CEO;<sup id="cite&#95;ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and Mikhail Seregine,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> a co-founder at <a href="/wiki/Outschool" title="Outschool">Outschool</a>.
</p><p>In late 2003, the concept for compute,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>e<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which would later launch as <a href="/wiki/Amazon_EC2" class="mw-redirect" title="Amazon EC2">EC2</a>, was reformulated when Chris Pinkham and Benjamin Black presented a paper internally describing a vision for Amazon's retail computing infrastructure that was completely standardized, completely automated, and would rely extensively on web services for services such as storage and would draw on internal work already underway. Near the end of their paper, they mentioned the possibility of selling access to virtual servers as a service, proposing the company could generate revenue from the new infrastructure investment.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-PinkhamBlack2003Paper&#95;48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PinkhamBlack2003Paper-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (February 2021)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Thereafter Pinkham, <a href="/wiki/Willem_van_Biljon" title="Willem van Biljon">Willem van Biljon</a>, and lead developer Christopher Brown developed the Amazon EC2 service, with a team in <a href="/wiki/Cape_Town" title="Cape Town">Cape Town</a>, South Africa.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In November 2004, AWS launched its first <a href="/wiki/IaaS" class="mw-redirect" title="IaaS">infrastructure service</a> for public usage: <a href="/wiki/Amazon_Simple_Queue_Service" title="Amazon Simple Queue Service">Simple Queue Service</a> (SQS).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="S3,_EC2,_and_other_first-generation_services_(2006–2010)"><span id="S3.2C_EC2.2C_and_other_first-generation_services_.282006.E2.80.932010.29"></span>S3, EC2, and other first-generation services (2006–2010)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amazon_Web_Services&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: S3, EC2, and other first-generation services (2006–2010)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1305433154">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox-move{border-left:10px solid #9932cc}.mw-parser-output .ambox-protection{border-left:10px solid #a2a9b1}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-text{border:none;padding:0.25em
